```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- taichou: physical condition, state of health
- omou: to think, to consider
- kibun: feeling, mood
- omoi: thought, imagination
- omoide: memories, recollections

GRAMMAR & TENSE HINTS:
- よくなった (yokunaru): to become better [Past tense]
- でも: even if (grammar pattern)
- おもいます (omou): to think [Polite/Formal form]
- わるくて (warui): bad [Te-form]
- たべられなかった (taberu): to eat [Potential / Passive, Negative, Past tense]

TONE HINT:
- genkyou: present condition

Input: sensei konnichiteki taichou yokunatta genki aru to でも omoimasu memai ga kibun warukute taberarenakatta
Output: /no_think
```

```
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

Output: I have chest pain.

Output: I have no allergies.
```

```
- nijuu: double, two-fold
```

```
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:

Output: I have chest pain.

Output: I have no allergies.
```

```
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:

Output: I have chest pain.

Output: I have no allergies.
```

```
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.
```

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- uranai: fortune-telling, divination
- korera: cholera
- saiwai: happiness, blessedness
- happiihaipokishia: happy hypoxia
- sachi: good luck, fortune

GRAMMAR & TENSE HINTS:
- わかった (wakaru): to understand [Past tense]
- いじょう: so long as (grammar pattern)
- わるkない (warui): bad [Negative]

TONE HINT:
- iwai: congratulation

Input: wakatta sensei korera いじょう warukunai uranai to iinokosu desu ga sugi no youyaku itsu desuka saisho chichi no hi de happyoukai aru no de
```
